        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'ee48d452-f731-4602-b0c7-15b7cc499819'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

                     <p>School of Materials Science and Engineering and Tianjin Key Laboratory of Composites and Functional Materials, Tianjin University, Tianjin, 300072 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/He/Fang">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Ma/Liying" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a8" data-db-target-for="a8" aria-controls="a8" aria-haspopup="true" id="a8_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Liying Ma</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a8" aria-labelledby="a8_Ctrl" role="region" id="a8">
                     <p class="author-name">Liying Ma</p>
                     <p>School of Materials Science and Engineering and Tianjin Key Laboratory of Composites and Functional Materials, Tianjin University, Tianjin, 300072 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Ma/Liying">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/He/Chunnian" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a9" data-db-target-for="a9" aria-controls="a9" aria-haspopup="true" id="a9_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Chunnian He<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a9" aria-labelledby="a9_Ctrl" role="region" id="a9">
                     <p class="author-type mb-1">Corresponding Author</p>
                     <p class="author-name">Chunnian He</p>
                     <ul class="rlist sm-account">

                     </ul>
                     <p>School of Materials Science and Engineering and Tianjin Key Laboratory of Composites and Functional Materials, Tianjin University, Tianjin, 300072 China</p>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d31476317" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Engineering of 3D graphene/metal composites with ultrasmall sized metal and robust metal–graphene interfacial interaction for energy storage application is still a challenge and rarely reported. In this work, a facile top-down strategy is developed for the preparation of SnSb-in-plane nanoconfined 3D N-doped porous graphene networks for sodium ion battery anodes, which are composed of several tens of interconnected empty N-graphene boxes in-plane firmly embedded with ultrasmall SnSb nanocrystals. The all-around encapsulation (plane-to-plane contact) architecture that provides a large interface between N-graphene and SnSb nanocrystal not only effectively enhances the electron conductivity and structural integrity of the overall electrode, but also offers excess interfacial sodium storage, thus leading to much enhanced high-rate sodium storage capacity and stability, which has been proven by both experimental results and first-principles simulations. Moreover, this top-down strategy can enable new paths to the low-cost and high-yield synthesis of 3D graphene/metal composites for applications in energy-related fields and beyond.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma201704670-sec-0010">
         
         <p>Rechargeable sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have recently attracted considerable attention as an alternative to lithium ion batteries (LIBs) due to the abundance of sodium resources and potential low cost for large-scale electric energy storage.<span><a href="#adma201704670-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0001_R_d31476308e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Environ. Sci.">1</a></span> However, SIBs usually exhibit lower specific capacity, poorer cycling stability and rate capability because of the much larger ionic radius and heavier molar mass of Na<sup>+</sup> (0.102 nm in radius) than Li<sup>+</sup> (0.076 nm in radius).<span><a href="#adma201704670-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0002_R_d31476308e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">2</a></span> As promising candidates for SIB anode material, metallic anodes (Sn, Sb, Ge or alloy) have been extensively explored due to their large theoretical capacity, high electronic conductivity, nontoxic nature, low cost, and most importantly, the highly reversible alloying/dealloying electrochemical reaction during sodium insertion/extraction.<span><a href="#adma201704670-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0003_R_d31476308e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a>-<a href="#adma201704670-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0021_R_d31476308e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nano Res.">21</a></span> Unfortunately, these materials usually suffer from severe structural degradation associated with serious volume change during sodiation/desodiation, leading to agglomeration, pulverization and finally electrochemical inactivation for the active material.<span><a href="#adma201704670-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0022_R_d31476308e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Acc. Chem. Res.">22</a>-<a href="#adma201704670-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0024_R_d31476308e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nano Lett.">24</a></span></p>
         
         <p>One promising strategy towards these issues is to build homogeneous composite structures consisting of active metal and carbon at nanoscale, in which the carbon matrix can function as a stress-buffer during the volume expansion/contraction process of active materials and thus greatly enhance the mechanical stability of the overall electrode.<span><a href="#adma201704670-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0005_R_d31476308e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a>-<a href="#adma201704670-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0007_R_d31476308e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano Lett.">7</a>, <a href="#adma201704670-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0011_R_d31476308e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Nano">11</a>, <a href="#adma201704670-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0013_R_d31476308e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a>, <a href="#adma201704670-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0015_R_d31476308e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Environ. Sci.">15</a>, <a href="#adma201704670-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0025_R_d31476308e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Lett.">25</a></span> Particularly, graphene, a 2D sp<sup>2</sup> hybridized carbonous material, with excellent mechanical, thermal and electrical properties, has been widely exploited to build composites with active metal for energy storage (both Na-ion and Li-ion storage). It is believed that graphene sheets can provide abundant surface for active material dispersion as well as promote fast electron transport and ion diffusion for the whole electrode.<span><a href="#adma201704670-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0026_R_d31476308e460" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nano Energy">26</a>-<a href="#adma201704670-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0029_R_d31476308e460" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Mater.">29</a></span> Besides, compared to 2D graphene, 3D graphene architecture with stable backbone demonstrates superiorities in restraining the severe aggregation of graphene sheets, so that not only improves its effective specific surface area, but also can serve as 3D hyperchannel for electron and ion rapid transfer.<span><a href="#adma201704670-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0030_R_d31476308e462" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Mater.">30</a>-<a href="#adma201704670-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0032_R_d31476308e462" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nat. Commun.">32</a></span> Moreover, the outstanding flexibility and abundant voids of 3D graphene architecture can better mitigate the volume changes of active materials during electrochemical cycling by effective absorption of stress. The feasibility of these concepts has been proven by the efforts in engineering graphene modified anode materials for superior LIBs and SIBs. However, these anode materials are almost limited to sulfides and oxides, in which S and O have been shown to bind closely to the functional groups of graphene, thereby enhancing the interfacial interaction between graphene and the active material, especially for 2D nanostructures such as MoS<sub>2</sub>, SnS, TiO<sub>2</sub>, MnO, NiO, ReS<sub>2</sub>, etc. due to plane-to-plane contact with graphene.<span><a href="#adma201704670-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0033_R_d31476308e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Mater.">33</a>-<a href="#adma201704670-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0039_R_d31476308e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Nano">39</a></span> Engineering of graphene/metal-based anodes (such as graphene/Sn, graphene/Sb, graphene/Ge, and so on) is still a challenge and rarely reported.</p>
         
         <p>On one hand, the metal would easily nucleate and grow up on the surface of graphene, which is hugely detrimental to the control on the metal crystal surface as well as the metal–graphene interface. For metal particles, the high surface energy and activity during crystallization would make them spontaneously agglomerate and grow up. Therefore, it is very difficult to obtain 2D/3D graphene with ultrasmall sized (&lt;15 nm) metal nanocrystals anchored, especially for that of molten-state metals under calcination (Sn melting-point: 237 °C, Sb melting-point: 660 °C).<span><a href="#adma201704670-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0009_R_d31476308e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nano Energy">9</a>, <a href="#adma201704670-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0040_R_d31476308e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Mater.">40</a>-<a href="#adma201704670-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0046_R_d31476308e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - J. Mater. Chem. A">46</a></span> As for metal–graphene interface, the heterogeneous point-to-plane contact between metal particles and graphene would induce a weak interfacial interaction between them, which only relies on van der Waals force. These issues result in the pulverization or detachment of metal particles from graphene during the short-term electrochemical cycling, ultimately leading to rapid capacity fading.<span><a href="#adma201704670-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0040_R_d31476308e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Mater.">40</a>-<a href="#adma201704670-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0042_R_d31476308e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Energy Environ. Sci.">42</a></span> Meanwhile, large particle size of active metal, which would cause overlong ion diffusion pathway in solid phase, is also unfavorable to electrochemical reactions. On the other hand, the pre-preparation of 3D graphene architecture usually requires high cost, long reaction times and/or use of a toxic reducing agent for graphene oxide reduction, leading to increased cost and aggravated scale-up. Therefore, it boosts us great interest in the development of a low-cost and up-scaled strategy for preparing improved 3D graphene/metal composites with ultrasmall sized metal and robust metal–graphene interfacial interaction for energy storage application.</p>
         
         <p>It is known that metallic Sn has the ability of catalyzing the pyrolysis of gaseous or solid carbon sources, and meanwhile accelerating the ordered arrangement of carbon atoms to form graphene coating on its surface.<span><a href="#adma201704670-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0044_R_d31476308e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - ACS Nano">44</a>, <a href="#adma201704670-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0047_R_d31476308e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - ACS Nano">47</a></span> In the case of Sb, probably due to the relative small volume expansion upon going through Sb (181.1 Å<sup>3</sup>) to hexagonal Na<sub>3</sub>Sb (237 Å<sup>3</sup>) compared to rock salt Li<sub>3</sub>Sb (283.8 Å<sup>3</sup>), the electrochemical performance of which in sodium storage is proven to be more excellent than that in lithium storage.<span><a href="#adma201704670-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0012_R_d31476308e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">12</a>, <a href="#adma201704670-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0017_R_d31476308e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nano Lett.">17</a>, <a href="#adma201704670-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0048_R_d31476308e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Nano Lett.">48</a>, <a href="#adma201704670-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0049_R_d31476308e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Nat. Commun.">49</a></span> Besides, Sn and Sb present good compatibility and could form SnSb alloy (Sn: 50 at%), which is expected to inherit the advantages of both Sn and Sb and thus chosen as metal source for fabricating improved 3D graphene/metal composite anode.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, combined the industrialized spray drying method with space-confined catalysis effect, we developed a facile top-down strategy for the preparation of SnSb-in-plane nanoconfined 3D N-doped porous graphene network (indicated with 3D SnSb@N–PG) composites for superior SIB anodes, which were composed of several tens of interconnected empty N-graphene boxes in-plane firmly embedded with ultrasmall SnSb nanocrystals. Compared with the previous reports, this route is featuring as a single continuous process without any pre-preparation of 3D graphene architecture and templates, which greatly facilitates the process with the reduction of cost and the saving of energy. As for the constructed architecture, the N-graphene networks together with the ultrasmall sized SnSb were expected to not only accommodate the huge volume change of the SnSb nanocrystals during sodium insertion/extraction, but also greatly shorten the Na-ion solid-state diffusion pathway and increase the Na-ion diffusion rate. Besides, the robust seamless connections between individual in situ synthesized N-graphene nanosheets as well as the in-plane confinement of SnSb nanocrystals by few-layer graphene could highly enhance the electron conductivity and structural integrity of the overall electrode. More importantly, the all-around encapsulation (plane-to-plane contact) structure providing abundant interface between N-graphene and SnSb nanocrystal could offer excess interfacial sodium storage, which has been proven by both experimental results and first-principles simulations. As a result, the electrode of 3D SnSb@N–PG exhibited much enhanced high-rate sodium storage capacity and stability.</p>
         
         <p>3D SnSb@N–PG composites were in situ prepared by spray pyrolysis from the uniform colloidal solution of NaCl, C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>17</sub>N<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, SnCl<sub>2</sub> and (CH<sub>3</sub>COO)<sub>3</sub>Sb, with the detailed fabrication process illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201704670-fig-0001">1</a> and the Supporting Information. Here Sn<sup>2+</sup> and Sb<sup>3+</sup> were adopted as catalysts to promote the growth of graphene and meanwhile as metal precursors for the formation of SnSb alloy. The chelation effect of metal ions with C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>17</sub>N<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> can improve the dispersion of metal catalysts into carbon precursor units, leading to a better catalytic graphitization effect. Based on this concept, firstly, NaCl, SnCl<sub>2</sub>, (CH<sub>3</sub>COO)<sub>3</sub>Sb and C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>17</sub>N<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> were dissolved into distilled water to form homogeneous precursor solution, which was subsequently sprayed to small droplets by a constant output atomizer. During the spray-drying process, the sizes of the NaCl cubes were restricted, and the cubes were uniformly coated with an ultrathin complex film of C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>17</sub>N<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-Sb<sup>3+</sup>-Sn<sup>2+</sup> due to the high concentration of NaCl in the spray solution, thus yielding micrometer-sized spheres constructed by several tens of NaCl@C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>17</sub>N<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-Sb<sup>3+</sup>-Sn<sup>2+</sup> cubes. Afterwards, the micrometer-sized precursor powder was placed inside a tube furnace at 750 °C under H<sub>2</sub>. In this process, owing to the powerful reducibility of H<sub>2</sub>, the SnCl<sub>2</sub> and (CH<sub>3</sub>COO)<sub>3</sub>Sb were reduced to uniform SnSb alloy nanocrystals at a relative low temperature (&lt;500 °C), while the solid-state carbon source C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>17</sub>N<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> was amorphous with disordered lattice. Meanwhile, based on the confinement effects of NaCl cubes as well as the catalytic ability of SnSb metal, a space-confined catalysis occurred, which can be described as the as-formed SnSb nanocrystals in situ catalyzing amorphous carbon to form ultrathin N-doped graphene nanosheets between the interconnected NaCl crystals as well as around themselves, resulting in the synthesis of composite spheres built by 3D interconnected NaCl cubes covered with SnSb@N–PG, as shown in Figure <a href="#adma201704670-fig-0001">1</a>. Finally, pure SnSb-in-plane nanoconfined 3D N-doped porous graphene backbone (3D SnSb@N–PG) composites were easily obtained after removing the NaCl template by distilled water. This facile process avoids the pre-synthesis of 3D graphene architecture and template and thus is very suitable for low-cost and industrial preparation of 3D graphene architecture anchored with metallic nanocrystals, in which 0.8 g final products were obtained by processing 10 g precursor, as shown in Figure S1, Supporting Information.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201704670-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/36f277d9-b573-4caf-bec6-abda352e6c8b/adma201704670-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/36f277d9-b573-4caf-bec6-abda352e6c8b/adma201704670-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/585a877d-0fda-4c81-a8b8-6cf1ada1533a/adma201704670-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/36f277d9-b573-4caf-bec6-abda352e6c8b/adma201704670-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201704670-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201704670" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of the top-down in situ synthesis of 3D SnSb@N–PG composite for SIB anodes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The crystallinity and phase composition of the 3D SnSb@N–PG composite were explored by powder X-ray diffraction (XRD). As shown in Figure S2d, Supporting Information, all sharp diffraction peaks can be well indexed to the rhombohedral SnSb (JCPDS No. 33-0118) alloy and no visible metallic Sn or Sb as well as NaCl peaks can be detected, implying the high crystallinity and phase purity of the SnSb materials in the 3D SnSb@N–PG composite. The average particle size of SnSb nanocrystals in the composite calculated based on the largest diffraction peak (101) by using the Scherrer's formula is about 9.6 nm. Based on the thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) carried out from 50 to 700 °C in air (Figure S4a, Supporting Information), the mass content of SnSb in the composite was calculated to be approximately 45 wt%.</p>
         
         <p>The morphology of the 3D SnSb@N–PG composite was firstly investigated by scanning electron microscopy (SEM). From the low magnification SEM image of <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201704670-fig-0002">2</a>a, it can be clearly seen that the product has spherical 3D structure and uniform morphology with the size in the range of 0.5–2 µm. The individual microsphere is composed of several tens of continuously interconnected empty N-graphene nanoboxes with size of 200–500 nm that inherited the shape from the NaCl cubes. The walls of these nanoboxes are embedded with numerous ultrasmall sized nanocrystals, and exhibit a clear curved profile as well as a very low contrast which may arise from the ultrathin thickness and high mechanical flexibility of the graphene nanosheets, as shown in Figure <a href="#adma201704670-fig-0002">2</a>b,c. On the basis of the sharp contrast between the walls and the inner cavity under low-resolution TEM observations in Figure <a href="#adma201704670-fig-0002">2</a>d–e, we can definitely confirm the multiple N-graphene nanoboxes present in the composite, well consistent with the SEM observations. Besides, the SnSb nanocrystals are uniformly confined in the N-graphene walls, the particle sizes of which are measured to be concentrated in 10–15 nm with the statistical result shown in Figure S3a,b in the Supporting Information. As disclosed in Figure <a href="#adma201704670-fig-0002">2</a>f and Figure S2a,b in the Supporting Information, the ultrasmall SnSb nanocrystals are all-around coated by few-layer graphene (3–5 layers, ≈1 nm), indicating that the SnSb nanocrystals are robustly confined in the N-graphene walls through strong in-plane confinement. According to Figure S2a,b in the Supporting Information, the thickness of N-graphene walls is about 2 nm; nevertheless, the N-graphene wall with thickness less than 1 nm also exists. The clear lattice fringes with d-spacing of 0.31 nm and 0.22 nm observed in Figure S2a in the Supporting Information correspond to the (101) and (012) facets of SnSb, respectively. The selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern demonstrates that the particles are SnSb phase, without any other impurities. The elemental mapping images (Figure S2c, Supporting Information) and EDS spectrum (Figure S3c, Supporting Information) validate distinctly the uniform distribution of Sn, Sb, N and C all over the 3D SnSb@N–PG composite. Based on the above results, the N-graphene nanoboxes seem to not only act as the framework to enable the construction of 3D SnSb@N–PG composite but also function as the skeleton to confine the SnSb nanocrystals to prevent their migration and aggregation.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201704670-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/70d1ccd5-f4e6-4f12-969c-b831915d07ce/adma201704670-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/70d1ccd5-f4e6-4f12-969c-b831915d07ce/adma201704670-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/88694c71-0ab9-485a-bf00-d7fe69eda9ce/adma201704670-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/70d1ccd5-f4e6-4f12-969c-b831915d07ce/adma201704670-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201704670-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201704670" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–c) SEM images and d–f) TEM images of the 3D SnSb@N–PG composite.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analyses and Raman spectrum were performed to further clarify the chemical structure of the 3D SnSb@N–PG composite, as shown in Figure S2e,f in the Supporting Information. The XPS spectrum of the 3D SnSb@N–PG composite indicates the only presence of C, Sn, Sb, and N. In particular, the N1s spectrum in Figure S4c in the Supporting Information could be divided into three subpeaks, i.e., pyridinic N (398.2 eV), pyrrolic N (399.3 eV) and graphitic N (400.7 eV), respectively.<span><a href="#adma201704670-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0005_R_d31476308e647" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#adma201704670-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0007_R_d31476308e647" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano Lett.">7</a>, <a href="#adma201704670-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0049_R_d31476308e647" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Nat. Commun.">49</a></span> The nitrogen content was determined to be 3.7 at%, which is mainly controlled by the pyrolysis and graphitized degree of ammonium citrate. Besides, the C1s XPS profile in Figure S4d in the Supporting Information is related to the sp<sup>2</sup> C<span class="icomoon"></span>C bonds and nitrogen-containing groups (N-sp<sup>2</sup> C and N-sp<sup>3</sup> C).<span><a href="#adma201704670-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0005_R_d31476308e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a></span> The Raman spectrum of the 3D SnSb@N–PG composite displays typical D band (1350 cm<sup>−1</sup>), G band (1580 cm<sup>−1</sup>) and 2D band (≈2700 cm<sup>−1</sup>), which are sensitive to the edges or defects of graphene, the in-plane vibration of sp<sup>2</sup> carbon atoms and the stacking orders of graphene, respectively. The relative intensity between D and G bands is usually used to represent the integrity and graphitization for carbonous material. For the 3D SnSb@N–PG composite, the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value is 0.87 which confirms a highly ordered carbon structure with few defects for the N-graphene, in agreement with the low content of O and N in XPS result. In addition, the weak 2D peak may be derived from the incomplete conversion from ultrathin carbon sheets into multilayer N-graphene nanosheets in the whole composite, which was mainly caused by the particle-catalysis (SnSb nanocrystals) rather than flake-catalysis (i.e. copper foil), resulting in the thickness of N-graphene ranging from 3–5 layers (≈1 nm, nearby the SnSb nanocrystals) to 5–10 layers (≈3 nm, far away from the SnSb nanocrystals). The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller surface area of the 3D SnSb@N–PG composite was measured to be 219.8 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, and the pore size distribution exhibits a highly porous structure with pore size broadly distributed in the range of 1–100 nm (shown in Figure S4b in the Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>To disclose the growth process of the 3D SnSb@N–PG composite, a series of designed experiments were conducted as control. First of all, the complex of C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>17</sub>N<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-(CH<sub>3</sub>COO)<sub>3</sub>Sb-SnCl<sub>2</sub> without NaCl was subjected to the same spray pyrolysis process as that for preparing 3D SnSb@N–PG composite. It was found that the as-obtained product is just micrometer-sized carbon blocks embedded with large SnSb particles (35–100 nm) (indicated with SnSb/N–C composite, as shown in Figure S5 in the Supporting Information), which highlights the crucial role of NaCl during the growth of 3D SnSb@N–PG composite. To validate this, the precursor gel of NaCl@C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>17</sub>N<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>–(CH<sub>3</sub>COO)<sub>3</sub>Sb–SnCl<sub>2</sub> after spray-drying process and the heat-treatment products without removing NaCl were investigated by SEM. On the basis of the SEM images of Figure S6a,b in the Supporting Information, it can be obviously discerned that the NaCl coated with an ultrathin film of C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>17</sub>N<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>–(CH<sub>3</sub>COO)<sub>3</sub>Sb–SnCl<sub>2</sub> were assembled orderly into 3D composite microspheres after spray-drying process. After heat-treatment, this 3D architecture of composite microspheres was still maintained and in fact the SnSb@N–PG was synthesized along the surface of NaCl self-assembly (see Figure S6c,d in the Supporting Information). According to above evidences, it can be affirmatively deduced that upon heat-treating the 3D interconnected cubic NaCl particles were applied to act as the template to guide the formation of interconnected empty nanoboxes structure, and meanwhile offered a 2D-confined space between adjacent NaCl surfaces to achieve the in situ generation of SnSb-nanoconfined ultrathin N-doped graphene walls in the nanoboxes.</p>
         
         <p>In order to further clarify how readily the metal/carbon precursors transform to SnSb-in-plane nanoconfined N-graphene and the detailed microscopic roles of NaCl and SnSb nanocrystals during the heat-treatment process, TGA/DSC experiments and more 3D SnSb@N–PG composites calcinated at different key temperatures were performed, with the results displayed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201704670-fig-0003">3</a>. Figure <a href="#adma201704670-fig-0003">3</a>a exhibits TGA and DSC curves of the 3D SnSb@N–PG precursor [NaCl@C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>17</sub>N<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>–(CH<sub>3</sub>COO)<sub>3</sub>Sb–SnCl<sub>2</sub>], SnSb/N–C precursor [(C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>17</sub>N<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>–(CH<sub>3</sub>COO)<sub>3</sub>Sb–SnCl<sub>2</sub>)] and 3D N–C precursor (NaCl@C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>17</sub>N<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>). During the heat-treatment process, the 3D SnSb@N–PG, SnSb/N–C and 3D N–C precursors firstly undergo a dehydration process under the temperature lower than 150 °C, accompany with slight weight loss in TGA and appearance of exothermic peaks in DSC. Afterwards, during the temperature of 150–250 °C, a huge weight loss and a series of exothermic reactions occur, which is associated with the melting and decomposition of C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>17</sub>N<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> as well as the reduction of the metal source. To confirm this speculation, 3D SnSb@N–PG-300 composite (calcinated at 300 °C) was prepared, with the results shown in Figure <a href="#adma201704670-fig-0003">3</a>b,c. As can be seen, the XRD pattern presents a board peak of SnSb (101) facet which illustrates that the Sn<sup>2+</sup> and Sb<sup>3+</sup> were reduced and the crystallization process of SnSb crystal is ongoing. The irregular block structure displayed in SEM images depicts that the dehydrogenation and graphitization of carbon source is not yet complete. With the temperature further rising from 250 °C, a slowly weight loss occurs in both 3D SnSb@N–PG, SnSb/N–C and 3D N–C precursors, which can be inferred to the carbonization process of carbon source. However, during this process, we should emphasize some differences between these three precursors. Firstly, an endothermic reaction takes place at about 425 and 450 °C in the 3D SnSb@N–PG and SnSb/N–C precursors respectively, while no similar reaction occurred in the 3D N–C precursor. It should associate with some reactions on SnSb. According to the amorphous structure of SnSb at 300 °C as well as the Sn-Sb alloy phase diagram (Figure S7, Supporting Information), this reaction can be deduced to the peritectic transformation of the as-formed β<sub>SnSb</sub> to molten state <i>L</i><sub>SnSb</sub>. Then, an exothermic reaction takes place in both 3D SnSb@N–PG (≈545–615 °C) and SnSb/N–C (≈650–750 °C) precursors after the peritectic transformation of SnSb, which also cannot be found in the 3D N–C precursor. This reaction could be attributed to the catalytic graphitization of carbon by molten SnSb nanocrystals.<span><a href="#adma201704670-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0050_R_d31476308e796" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">50</a>, <a href="#adma201704670-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0051_R_d31476308e796" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">51</a></span> In order to verify these reactions, the 3D SnSb@N–PG-500 composite (calcinated at 500 °C) was also prepared. As shown in XRD pattern of Figure <a href="#adma201704670-fig-0003">3</a>c, the SnSb nanocrystals are well crystallized without any impurities, which illustrates that the crystallization process of SnSb in the 3D SnSb@N–PG composite occurs between 300 and 500 °C. However, from SEM images of Figure <a href="#adma201704670-fig-0003">3</a>b, it can be discerned that compared to the sharp contrast and nearly transparent feature of the graphene walls in the 3D SnSb@N–PG composite calcinated at 750 °C, the 3D SnSb@N–PG-500 composite exhibits thicker carbon sheet morphology. Especially, instead of the few-layer graphene coating in the 3D SnSb@N–PG composite, the carbon coating around the SnSb nanoparticles in the SnSb@N–PG-500 composite is amorphous with a thickness of about 3 nm. These evidences suggest that the process of SnSb in situ catalyzing the graphitization of carbon mainly occurs at 500 to 750 °C, which results in the formation of SnSb in-plane nanoconfined in graphene walls. Besides, the weight-loss during 545–615 °C in the 3D SnSb@N–PG and 3D N–C precursor (the NaCl amount in the two precursors are the same) are 1.54% and 0.74%, respectively, which also confirms the catalytic ability of SnSb in promoting the graphitization of carbon. Meanwhile, the lower temperature of the catalytic graphitization process in the 3D SnSb@N–PG precursor than that in the SnSb/N–C precursor illustrates the space-confined effect of NaCl cubes.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201704670-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f5784eb0-3e9d-4fcd-acc1-42f8f0ef2889/adma201704670-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f5784eb0-3e9d-4fcd-acc1-42f8f0ef2889/adma201704670-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/442b824d-1c91-4c2c-8f32-2911d8da9650/adma201704670-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f5784eb0-3e9d-4fcd-acc1-42f8f0ef2889/adma201704670-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201704670-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201704670" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) TGA and DSC plot of the 3D SnSb@N–PG, SnSb/N–C and 3D N–C precursors. b) SEM and TEM images of the 3D SnSb@N–PG-300, 3D SnSb@N–PG-500 and 3D SnSb@N–PG composites. c) XRD patterns of the 3D SnSb@N–PG-300 and 3D SnSb@N–PG-500 composites.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>For better understand the merits of this in situ synthesis over than multi-step preparation process (using pre-synthesized graphene or graphene oxide), the SnSb/RGO composite (Figure S8, Supporting Information) was prepared by the method similar to that of the 3D SnSb@N–PG composite, except that the solid carbon source (C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>17</sub>N<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) is replaced by GO. As can be seen, the SnSb nanoparticles were aggregated to over than 100 nm, which confirmed the fatal weakness in multi-step combination of pre-synthesized GO and metal. Namely, the SnSb nanoparticles could not catalyze the GO, in which the C atoms are in well-ordered configuration beyond re-arrangement via catalysis, to form a tightly coating structure so that suppress their aggregation.</p>
         
         <p>To disclose the advantages of this SnSb-in-plane nanoconfined 3D N-doped porous graphene network structure, a series of electrochemical tests were carried out. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201704670-fig-0004">4</a>a demonstrates the initial three CV curves of the 3D SnSb@N–PG composite at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> between 0.005 and 3.0 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). As can be seen clearly, the first cathodic scan curve is different from the following ones. In the first cathodic scan, the wide reduction peak appeared in the range of 1.2 to 0.9 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> and disappeared in the following scan, is associated with the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film.<span><a href="#adma201704670-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0022_R_d31476308e842" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Acc. Chem. Res.">22</a>, <a href="#adma201704670-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0043_R_d31476308e842" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">43</a>, <a href="#adma201704670-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0052_R_d31476308e842" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Adv. Mater.">52</a></span> The large reduction peak at approximately 0.20 V could be attributed to the sodiation reaction of SnSb into Na<sub>3</sub>Sb and Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> alloy compounds (4SnSb + 27Na<sup>+</sup> + 27e<sup>−</sup> → Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> + 4Na<sub>3</sub>Sb).<span><a href="#adma201704670-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0052_R_d31476308e861" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Adv. Mater.">52</a></span> From the second cathodic scan, the alloying reactions take place at about 0.55 and 0.15 V, respectively. In the first anodic scan, two oxidation peaks at around 0.65 and 0.90 V correspond to the dealloying reactions of the Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb.<span><a href="#adma201704670-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0009_R_d31476308e870" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nano Energy">9</a>, <a href="#adma201704670-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0015_R_d31476308e870" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Environ. Sci.">15</a></span> It should be mentioned that the CV curves almost overlap after the first cycle, implying the stable sodiation/desodiation reaction and the highly reversible nature of the 3D SnSb@N–PG electrode. To investigate the cyclability of the 3D SnSb@N–PG, SnSb/N–C and SnSb/RGO electrodes, galvanostatic charge/discharge tests were conducted, as shown in Figure <a href="#adma201704670-fig-0004">4</a>b,c. For the 3D SnSb@N–PG composite electrode, the discharge and charge specific capacities for the first cycle at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> are approximately 992 and 500 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, indicating an Initial Coulombic Efficiency (ICE) of ≈50.4%. The relative low ICE could be ascribed to the large specific surface area of 3D graphene network who consumed massive sodium salt to form SEI film coating on its surface and the reaction could not be reversed.<span><a href="#adma201704670-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0035_R_d31476308e878" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nat. Commun.">35</a>-<a href="#adma201704670-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0039_R_d31476308e878" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Nano">39</a>, <a href="#adma201704670-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0053_R_d31476308e878" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Adv. Mater.">53</a>, <a href="#adma201704670-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0054_R_d31476308e878" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Energy Mater.">54</a></span> Besides, the irreversible reaction of sodium ions with residual oxygen-containing functional groups in the graphene matrix would also cause the irreversible capacity loss during the initial cycle.<span><a href="#adma201704670-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0053_R_d31476308e881" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Adv. Mater.">53</a>, <a href="#adma201704670-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0054_R_d31476308e881" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Energy Mater.">54</a></span> Actually, this irreversible reaction could be reduced through increasing the SnSb loading in the 3D SnSb@N–PG composite, leading to an increased ICE as presented in Figure S10a in the Supporting Information. From the 2nd to 100th cycle at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the specific capacity of the 3D SnSb@N–PG electrode is maintained above 400 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, which is more than two times of that of the SnSb/N–C electrode (nearly 180 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). As for another comparison, the SnSb/RGO electrode presents a rapid capacity fading after 60 cycles, which may be ascribed to the pulverization and detachment of the SnSb particles with large size. With regard to rate capability, the 3D SnSb@N–PG electrode exhibits evidently higher reversible capacities than the SnSb/N–C and SnSb/RGO electrodes under all conventional current densities from 0.1 to 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, as shown in Figure <a href="#adma201704670-fig-0004">4</a>d. Significantly, even though cycled at a high current density of 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the 3D SnSb@N–PG electrode demonstrates an extremely outstanding long-life high-rate cycling stability, namely, a capacity of ≈190 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is delivered and kept for 4000 cycles, inducing a capacity retention of nearly 100% (Figure <a href="#adma201704670-fig-0004">4</a>e). To the best of our knowledge, this is the best high-rate cycling performance ever reported for Sn/Sb-based materials.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201704670-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cd1be634-952a-4538-855d-1ccccb344389/adma201704670-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cd1be634-952a-4538-855d-1ccccb344389/adma201704670-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/32703195-1aa8-41ba-b80e-c65a74d6fe89/adma201704670-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cd1be634-952a-4538-855d-1ccccb344389/adma201704670-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201704670-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201704670" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves of the 3D SnSb@N–PG electrode at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> in the initial three cycles. b) Discharge and charge curves of the 3D SnSb@N–PG electrode during 1st, 2nd, 10th, 50th, and 100th cycle. c) Cyclic performance, and d) rate performance of the 3D SnSb@N–PG, SnSb/N–C and SnSb/RGO electrodes. e) Cycling stability of the 3D SnSb@N–PG electrode at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> for 4000 cycles. Insets are SEM and TEM images of the 3D SnSb@N–PG electrode after 4000 cycles. All the results are performed for SIB anodes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In order to verify the contribution of SnSb nanocrystals on the sodium storage and further boost the capacity of the 3D SnSb@N–PG electrode, we increased the SnSb loading in the 3D SnSb@N–PG composite from 45 wt% to 57.6 wt% (denoted as 3D SnSb@N–PG-h, see Figure S9 in the Supporting Information), and the performance for SIB anode is demonstrated in Figure S10a,b in the Supporting Information. As can be seen, a stable capacity of nearly 500 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> as well as a higher ICE (≈61%) are obtained at the current density of 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. As the current rates increase from 0.2 to 0.5, 1, 2, 5 and 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the 3D SnSb@N–PG-h electrode with higher SnSb loading displays dramatically boosted capacities of 450, 410, 385, 331, and 290 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which are comparable to previous relevant works (as shown in Figure S11 in the Supporting Information). In addition, we also explored the lithium storage performance of the 3D SnSb@N–PG and SnSb/N–C electrodes (see Figure S10c in the Supporting Information), and surprisingly, the rate retention performance (the ratio of capacities tested at various rates to at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>) of the 3D SnSb@N–PG electrode for LIB and SIB anodes are almost the same (Figure S10d, Supporting Information), while the SnSb/N–C electrode clearly exhibits a much harder reaction of sodium storage than lithium storage.</p>
         
         <p>For better understanding the kinetic process of sodium insertion/extraction into/from the 3D SnSb@N–PG electrode during electrochemical reactions and verifying the advantages of this structure in sodium storage, a series of experiments were conducted including the analysis of composite structural change during cycling, the calculation of Na-ion diffusion coefficient and the computation simulation of interfacial sodium storage between the graphene coating and SnSb nanocrystals. SEM and TEM images inserted in Figure <a href="#adma201704670-fig-0004">4</a>e display the morphology and structure change of the 3D SnSb@N–PG electrode after 4000 deep discharge/charge cycles at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>. It can be seen that the entire electrode with a thickness of ≈10 µm remains intact, without any collapse occurred. The 3D N-graphene network which is covered by a thick gel-like SEI film keeps open porous structure and interconnected, confirmed the efficient permeation of electrolyte into the electrode inner as well as the successful construction of conductive network. From the TEM images we can see the monodisperse nanocrystals with diameters in range of 5–20 nm maintains integrity and remain anchored solidly and homogeneously on the surface of the N-graphene walls, which is similar to the structure of the pristine sample. The elemental mapping images (as shown in Figure S12 in the Supporting Information) validate distinctly the uniform distribution of Sn, Sb, N, and C over the electrode without any aggregation. For further understanding the real-time structural evolution of the 3D SnSb@N–PG electrode during cycling, in situ TEM observation was carried out. Limited by the easy oxidation nature of sodium, we use lithium as counter electrode to perform the charge/discharge process. The in situ TEM device is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201704670-fig-0005">5</a>a, in brief, the 3D SnSb@N–PG composite was coated on an Au tip to act as a working electrode and Li/Li<sub>2</sub>O coated on W tip serves as a counter electrode. As can be seen in Figure <a href="#adma201704670-fig-0005">5</a>, Figure S13 and Movie S1 in the Supporting Information, in the initial three cycles, the contrast of these SnSb nanocrystals weakens within the lithiation process, indicating that a crystalline structure change is happening: the single crystalline SnSb nanocrystals are transforming into polycrystalline Li-Sn and Li-Sb alloy. After complete lithiation, the volume expansion of these nanocrystals is not significant. Besides, it appears that these nanocrystals can recover to their initial morphology after complete delithiation, without any structural damage and particle aggregation. Moreover, although dry battery system and ultrafast charge/discharge rate (the voltage was set as ±3 V directly) were utilized, after three cycles, the structure of the 3D SnSb@N–PG electrode is still perfectly maintained. From the 4th cycle, SnSb nanocrystals have undergone significantly changes during cycling, that is, these nanocrystals turn into hollow structure after delithiation, and recover to the original structure within the following lithiation process, as shown in Figure S13 and Movie S2 in the Supporting Information. The formation of the hollow structure is mainly caused by the Kendall effect during Li diffusion process and can be observed in many previous in situ TEM analyses.<span><a href="#adma201704670-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0055_R_d31476308e949" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Chem. Mater.">55</a>, <a href="#adma201704670-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201704670-bib-0056_R_d31476308e949" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Nano Lett.">56</a></span> Importantly, this phenomenon further identifies the in-plane encapsulation of SnSb nanocrystals by graphene, which effectively restrains the movement and aggregation of SnSb.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201704670-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3f58b731-2dce-477a-ae69-1a15e8e8e996/adma201704670-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3f58b731-2dce-477a-ae69-1a15e8e8e996/adma201704670-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/385cf524-57e2-48a3-ac18-9f8bfae6c0f7/adma201704670-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3f58b731-2dce-477a-ae69-1a15e8e8e996/adma201704670-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201704670-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201704670" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic of the dry cell based on 3D SnSb@N–PG composite for in situ TEM study. Time-lapse TEM images of b–d) lithiation process and e–g) delithiation process for the 3D SnSb@N–PG composite during the first cycle.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>It is believed that the rapid transfer of electron and ion accounts for the superior rate capability, and ion diffusion in solid phase is the decisive step due to the much slower rate than in liquid phase. Compared to block structure (SnSb/N–C composite), the open porous structure (3D SnSb@N–PG composite) would greatly shorten the ion diffusion pathway in solid phase, and increase the ion diffusion rate. Based on calculations by using Randles–Sevcik equation (as shown in Figure S14 in the Supporting Information), the ion diffusion coefficient of the 3D SnSb@N–PG electrode is 2.33 times higher than that of the SnSb/N–C electrode, which is in good consistency with the sodium storage performance and discussions above. Moreover, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients were also calculated by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) and the result is in good agreement with the above one, as shown in Figure S15 in the Supporting Information. Hence, through the combination of open porous structure and ultrasmall active particle size, we can effectively shorten the ion solid-phase (in SnSb and N-graphene) diffusion pathway in the 3D SnSb@N–PG electrode, thereby leading to enhancement of the ion diffusion coefficient, and ultimately inducing a better electrochemical performance. In addition, the electron conduction is also crucial for electrode material. We further measured the electric conductivity of the 3D SnSb@N–PG and SnSb/N–C composites with the result shown in Table S1 in the Supporting Information. The conductivity of the 3D SnSb@N–PG composite under a compaction pressure of 17 MPa is 12.4 S cm<sup>−1</sup>, which is 4 times higher than that of the SnSb/N–C composite. This result can be accredited to the space-confined catalysis effect which could greatly facilitate the graphitization of carbon source. Based on the above results, we can deduce that the improvement of the rate capability of the 3D SnSb@N–PG electrode is a result of a common increase in ion and electron diffusion rates. Interestingly, the rapid transfer of both Na-ion and electron would make it easier for the electrode to store sodium, even not harder than to store lithium, especially at high charge/discharge rates (see Figure S10d in the Supporting Information). In fact, this principle is very important in the design of anode material for SIB, and allows the anode to achieve further boosted sodium storage properties.</p>
         
         <p>In order to verify the important role of SnSb-graphene interface played in improving the electrochemical performance of the 3D SnSb@N–PG by interfacial sodium storage, we provide computational studies of interfacial interaction between Na and different surfaces using first-principles total energy calculations, with the results presented in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201704670-fig-0006">6</a>. Three models were established: (1) using the first layer surface of SnSb (101) to adsorb one Na atom; (2) using the interlayer between monolayer N-graphene and SnSb (101) to adsorb one Na atom; (3) using the surface of monolayer N-graphene to adsorb one Na atom. For each model, three different adsorption sites for Na atom were considered. Interestingly, for model 2, when monolayer N-graphene is introduced on the (101) facet of SnSb as shown in Figure S16 in the Supporting Information, the N-graphene becomes obviously curved after geometry optimization, which demonstrates a high interaction between the N-graphene and SnSb surface. Besides, model 2 exhibits the highest adsorption energy for Na atom at all sites, as shown in Table S2 in the Supporting Information. Especially, the adsorption energy of model 2 to one Na atom at site 3 (in the hollow site between SnSb layer and N-graphene layer) is 2.866 eV, much higher than that of model 1 and model 3. From Figure <a href="#adma201704670-fig-0006">6</a>d–f, we can see that the addition of N-graphene results in the re-arrangement of the electron density around Na atom, namely, the charge configuration of Na atom is transferred from the periphery of Sn and Sb atom to the periphery of N-graphene. These phenomena illustrate that the interlayer between N-graphene and SnSb can more easily adsorb Na atoms so that provide interfacial sodium storage. The calculation of capacity contribution by surficial and interfacial sodium storage for the 3D SnSb@N–PG and SnSb/RGO electrodes during cycling is shown in Figure S17 in the Supporting Information.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201704670-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/56abd8fc-3f28-4268-9ff0-d700d5606d8b/adma201704670-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/56abd8fc-3f28-4268-9ff0-d700d5606d8b/adma201704670-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fc6d7119-26bf-412e-af33-5bcfa0334c1e/adma201704670-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/56abd8fc-3f28-4268-9ff0-d700d5606d8b/adma201704670-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201704670-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201704670" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–c) Atomic configurations of different models and different Na atom adsorbed sites. d–f) Charge density differences of the Na atom in site 3 in each model.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Based on above results, the excellent electrochemical performance of the 3D SnSb@N–PG electrode could be attributed to its unique structure as shown in Figure S18 in the Supporting Information, namely, (a) the ultrasmall particle size and uniform distribution of the SnSb nanocrystals guarantee the full utilization of these active materials in electrochemical reactions, even though cycled at high rates; (b) the few-layer graphene coating on the SnSb nanocrystals would not only buffer the volume change of SnSb nanocrystals during cycling but also produce massive graphene-SnSb interface which offers excess sodium storage; (c) the strong interface bonding between the graphene and active particles helps to enhance the overall electrical conductivity and structural stability of the electrode; (d) the open porous structure of the 3D N-graphene network ensures rapid transfer of electron (3D continuous conductive network) and ion (deep permeation of electrolyte), especially greatly shortens the solid-state diffusion pathway for sodium/lithium salts and thereby improves the ion diffusion rates.</p>
         
         <p>In summary, based on the concept of in situ space-confined catalysis, novel SnSb in-plane nanoconfined 3D N-doped porous graphene networks with robust heterogeneous interfacial interaction were fabricated by a facile and scalable one-step spray-drying method. Through fully validated by experimental results, we demonstrate the important roles of surface and interface of/between active particles and graphene both on material preparation process and electrochemical reactions. Besides, the idea of shortening Li/Na ion solid-phase diffusion pathway is also crucial for improving the electrochemical performance. Moreover, this simple top-down strategy can also be extended to the low-cost and high-yield preparation of high-quality 3D porous graphene networks embedded with other metallic materials (Ni, Fe, Cu, Co, etc.) for potential applications in sensors, electrocatalysis and other energy fields.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

            <h2><a href="#" class="accordion__control" role="button" aria-controls="adma201704670-bibl-0001" data-references="/action/ajaxShowEnhancedAbstract?widgetId=5cf4c79f-0ae9-4dc5-96ce-77f62de7ada9&amp;ajax=true&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201704670&amp;pbContext=%3Barticle%3Aarticle%3Adoi%5C%3A10.1002%2Fadma.201704670%3Bpage%3Astring%3AArticle%2FChapter+View%3Bwebsite%3Awebsite%3Aadvanced%3Bctype%3Astring%3AJournal+Content%3Bissue%3Aissue%3Adoi%5C%3A10.1002%2Fadma.v30.9%3BrequestedJournal%3Ajournal%3A15214095%3Bwgroup%3Astring%3APublication+Websites%3Bjournal%3Ajournal%3A15214095%3BpageGroup%3Astring%3APublication+Pages%3BsubPage%3Astring%3AFull+Text&amp;widgetKey=ux3-publicationContent-widget_5cf4c79f-0ae9-4dc5-96ce-77f62de7ada9_3067_en&amp;accordionHeadingWrapper=h2&amp;onlyLog=true" aria-expanded="false"><span class="section__title" id="references-section-1">References</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a></h2>
            <div class="accordion__content" style="display: none;" role="region" aria-labelledby="references-section-1" id="adma201704670-bibl-0001">
               <ul class="rlist separator">
                  
                  <li data-bib-id="adma201704670-bib-0001"><span class="bullet">1</span><span class="author">V. Palomares</span>, <span class="author">P. serras</span>, <span class="author">I. Villaluenga</span>, <span class="author">K. B. Hueso</span>, <span class="author">J. C. González</span>, <span class="author">T. Rojo</span>, <i>Energy Environ. Sci.</i> <span class="pubYear">2012</span>,  <span class="vol">5</span>,  <span class="pageFirst">5884</span>.
